[09/11/2023, 1:32:03â€¯PM] ~â€¯Rayyan Alam: PERSONALITY
~ The characteristics and patterns of thoughts, feelings and behaviours that make a person unique
Comes from the Latin word persona, which refers to a theatrical mask worn by performers to disguise their true identity

Examples of thinking, feeling, and behaviours
Thinking: e.g. Optimistic vs Pessimistic
Feeling: e.g. Experience intense emotions or not
Behaving: e.g. Overtalking vs being closed up

Personality doesn't include:
- Physical and Biological Characteristics
- How people are when they are at their best
- Fleeting states like hunger, arousal or mood

Important Discussions
- Are you the same person online?
- How does social media influence our personality?
- How does our personality influence our use of social media
- What are online bullies like?

///////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////
THEORIES OF PERSONALITY

Psychoanalysis Theory (Frued)
~ According to Freud our personality is in conflict between 3 structures:
Ego, Superego, Id

Id (Reservoir of Psychic Energy)
- Works on the pleasure principle
- Source of all drives and urges
- The id seeks instant gratification
- We are all born with the id
Pleasure-based

Ego (Executive of Personality)
- Works on the reality principle
- Mediates between the id, superego and the environment
- Developed after 3 years of birth
Consequence-based

Superego (Upholder of Values and Principles)
- To do with values, morals and principles
- Often equated with the conscience
- Developed by our interactions with the world and caregivers
- Develops around age 5
Principle-based

Freud also described 3 levels of consciousness
Conscious, Sub-Conscious, Unconcious

Healthy Personality
In a healthy person, the ego is the strongest
If id is too strong it leads to selfishness
If superego is too strong it leads to being judgemental and unbending

Social Learning Theory
~ Behaviourism is the theory that people's behaviour 
is the result of the rewards and punishments they have experienced in the past
According to behaviourism, everyone has experienced different patterns of rewards and punishments
and thus developed different behaviours and personalities

Bandura's Social Learning Theory
Suggests we learn social behaviour by observing and imitating the behaviour of others
Suggest we learn from observing other people being rewarded or punished
Suggests everyone may interpret behaviour differently but on that basis learn
And that learning shapes our own behaviour and personalities 

Carl Roger's Humanistic Theory
~ Humanism is the theory that to understand a person 
is to understand how the person understands themselves (from their eyes we see them)
Roger's proposed a person-centered theory that states:
- Every person is unique
- People are inherently good and creative
- All people possess an inherent need to grow and achieve their potential
- People are free to choose their own lives and make their own decisions

Therefore for a personality to grow:
- Genuineness	being honest and open
- Acceptance of feelings and conflicts
- Empathy (being listened to and understood)
- Unconditional positive regard from the people you care most about
(especially from parents when you are young)

Concept of Self
- How someone percieves themselves
- Comprised of the real and ideal self
- When there is a large overlap of the ideal and real self then self-actualisation becomes easier

Maslow's Hierarchy of Needs
- Human beings have needs in a hierarchal fashion 
and when one need is achieved one can transcend to another level
Physiological Needs, Safety, Love and Belonging, Self-esteem, Self-Actualisation

Trait Theory
~ The theory suggests that we all have certain distinguishable personality traits
on the basis of which we can identify and group people and adhere to that group's needs
Personality Trait
- A stable, enduring quality that a person shows in most situations
- Extremely useful for describing personality
- Can we used to measure how people differ from others

5 Factor Model
~ is a personality test based on the assumption that 
personality can be sufficiently described by 5 traits

1 Openness
+ imagination, insight, curious, broad range of interests
- practical, conventional, follows routine

2 Conscientiousness
+ thoughtfulness, good impulse control, goal-directed behaviour
- impulsive, careless, 	disorganised

3 Extraversion
+ excitability, sociability, talkativeness, assertiveness, high amounts of expressiveness
- quiet, reserved, withdrawn

4 Agreeableness
+ trust, altruism, kindness, willingness to conform to group norms, affection
- critical, uncooperative, suspicious

5 Neuroticism
+ emotional instability, anxiety, moodiness, irritability
- calm, even-tempered, secure

PERSONALITY HAS CONSQUENCES
Much research has been done to connect personalities with consequences
- People with certain personality traits tend to do better in academic and career life
- People with certain personality traits tend to be better at relationships



///////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////////
DEFENCE MECHANISMS
~ Unconscious psychological operation that functions to protect a person 
from anxiety-producing thoughts and feelings related to internal conflicts and outer stressors

1 Denial
- Refusal to accept reality
e.g. Not accepting doctors diagnosis of cancer

2 Displacement
- Taking out negative emotions on a less threatening target
e.g. Domestic abuse due to a toxic work environment

3 Projection
- Placing one's own unacceptable thoughts onto others
e.g. Feeling stupid but calling others stupid

4 Regression
- Returning to a previous stage of development
e.g. sitting in a corner and crying

5 Repression
- Pushing threatening or conflicting events out of conscious memory
e.g. Forgetting about the math exam in grade 12 that went horribly

6 Sublimation
- Acting out acceptable impulses in a socially acceptable way
e.g. Exercising more after a stressful workday

7 Rationalisation
- Making up acceptable excuses for unacceptable behaviour
e.g. Saying you were angry and shouted at your colleagues because you were sick yesterday

8 Humor
- Focusing on the humorous aspect of an uncomfortable or adverse situation
e.g. Making biryani jokes at a loved one's funeral

9 Suppression
- Voluntarily exclusion Pushing threatening or conflicting events from awareness
e.g. Not talking about an event and avoiding subjects related to it

[26/12/2023, 8:08:45â€¯PM] ~â€¯<\>: A)
Performance optimization technique based on the overlap of the 
execution of multiple instructions deriving from a sequential 
execution flow

Add  R1,R2,R3
1) Fetch the instruction from memory
2) instruction decode ( identify operands R2,R3 and operators addition)
3) operand fetch ( the values of operands fetch to perform the  )
4) execute perform the addition
5) write back ( store result in R1)
[26/12/2023, 8:30:37â€¯PM] ~â€¯<\>: B
i) A hazard is created whenever there is a 
dependence between instructions, and instructions are 
close enough that the overlap caused by pipelining would 
change the order of access to the operands involved in 
the dependence.
Example 
Sw R1, 8(R9)
Sub R4,R1, R5

ii) data hazard is between line 2 and 3

[19/05/2024, 5:30:46â€¯PM] Mnp10: 1.	Page No (specific)= address(given)/ page size 
2.	Offset in page = address â€“ (page no.)(page size)
3.	Internal fragmentation = Page size â€“ Offset in page
4.	No of bits (x) => 2x {power of 2 represent no of bits}
5.	Logical address space = No of pages (2x) * Size of page (2y) == 2x+y
6.	Page table entries (No of pages)= logical address space (2x) / page size (2y) == 2x-y
7.	Physical address space = No of frames (2x) * Size of page (2y) == 2x+y
8.	Effective access time = (1-p)*memory access time + p* page fault service time. (p=probability of page fault)
9.	Physical address =  (frame no. * page size) + OFFSET 
Any other formulas from these chapters?

[19/05/2024, 7:28:04â€¯PM] ~â€¯Ayesha Nasir: 1.	Page No (specific)= address(given)/ page size 
2.	Offset in page = address â€“ (page no.)(page size)
3.	Internal fragmentation = Page size â€“ Offset in page
4.	No of bits (x) => 2x {power of 2 represent no of bits}
5.	Logical address space = No of pages (2x) * Size of page (2y) == 2x+y
6.	Page table entries (No of pages)= logical address space (2x) / page size (2y) == 2x-y
7.	Physical address space = No of frames (2x) * Size of page (2y) == 2x+y
8.	Effective access time = (1-p)*memory access time + p* page fault service time. (p=probability of page fault)
9.	Physical address =  (frame no. * page size) + OFFSET

[21/05/2024, 9:59:21â€¯AM] ~â€¯Anas Ashfaq Siddiqui: First thing to check: If Population's variance/sd given -> use Z.

Now if the let's say population variance/sd is not given it is understood that we have to use T. But there is assumption that we make, that is :
If sample size is too large we will not consider it sample instead it will behave like population so we will use it's variance/sd as if it was population's variance. So therefore replace T with Z.

[25/05/2024, 2:05:41â€¯PM] ~â€¯Muhammad Fasih: Prerequisite â€“ Turing Machine

Variants of Turing Machine 

1. Multiple track Turing Machine:
 A k-track Turing machine(for some k>0) has k-tracks and one R/W head that reads and writes all of them one by one.
A k-track Turing Machine can be simulated by a single track Turing machine

2. Two-way infinite Tape Turing Machine:
Infinite tape of two-way infinite tape Turing machine is unbounded in both directions left and right.
Two-way infinite tape Turing machine can be simulated by one-way infinite Turing machine(standard Turing machine).

3. Multi-tape Turing Machine:
It has multiple tapes and is controlled by a single head.
The Multi-tape Turing machine is different from k-track Turing machine but expressive power is the same.
Multi-tape Turing machine can be simulated by single-tape Turing machine.

4. Multi-tape Multi-head Turing Machine:
The multi-tape Turing machine has multiple tapes and multiple heads
Each tape is controlled by a separate head
Multi-Tape Multi-head Turing machine can be simulated by a standard Turing machine.

5. Multi-dimensional Tape Turing Machine:
It has multi-dimensional tape where the head can move in any direction that is left, right, up or down.
Multi dimensional tape Turing machine can be simulated by one-dimensional Turing machine

6. Multi-head Turing Machine:
A multi-head Turing machine contains two or more heads to read the symbols on the same tape.
In one step all the heads sense the scanned symbols and move or write independently.
Multi-head Turing machine can be simulated by a single head Turing machine.

7. Non-deterministic Turing Machine:
A non-deterministic Turing machine has a single, one-way infinite tape.
For a given state and input symbol has at least one choice to move (finite number of choices for the next move), each choice has several choices of the path that it might follow for a given input string.
A non-deterministic Turing machine is equivalent to the deterministic Turing machine.

[01/06/2024, 11:07:33â€¯PM] ~â€¯Anas Ashfaq Siddiqui: To remove useless productions from the given context-free grammar (CFG), we need to identify and eliminate productions that do not contribute to deriving terminal strings. We do this in two steps: identifying non-generating symbols (symbols that do not derive any terminal string) and non-reachable symbols (symbols that cannot be reached from the start symbol).

Given CFG:
```
S â†’ AxA | By | zC
A â†’ B | a
B â†’ C | b
C â†’ SS | c | Î» | ZBC
Z â†’ aBZ | BC | Z
```

### Step 1: Identify Generating Symbols
We need to identify which non-terminals can generate terminal strings.

- **Terminal symbols** (a, b, c, y, z) are generating by definition.
- **A â†’ a**: A is generating since it directly produces a terminal.
- **B â†’ b**: B is generating since it directly produces a terminal.
- **C â†’ c**: C is generating since it directly produces a terminal.
- **C â†’ Î»**: C is generating since it directly produces the empty string (Î»).

Using the productions that contain only generating symbols:
- **A â†’ B**: A is generating because B is generating.
- **B â†’ C**: B is generating because C is generating.
- **S â†’ zC**: S is generating because z is a terminal and C is generating.
- **C â†’ ZBC**: C is generating because Z, B, and C are generating.
- **Z â†’ aBZ**: Z is generating because a is terminal, B and Z are generating.
- **Z â†’ BC**: Z is generating because B and C are generating.

Now, all non-terminals (S, A, B, C, Z) are generating.

### Step 2: Identify Reachable Symbols
We need to identify which non-terminals can be reached starting from the start symbol S.

- Start with S.
- From **S â†’ AxA | By | zC**, we can reach A, B, C.
- From **A â†’ B | a**, we can reach B.
- From **B â†’ C | b**, we can reach C.
- From **C â†’ SS | c | Î» | ZBC**, we can reach S, Z, B.
- From **Z â†’ aBZ | BC | Z**, we can reach B.

So, all non-terminals (S, A, B, C, Z) are reachable from S.

### Conclusion
Since all non-terminals are both generating and reachable, there are no useless productions in the given CFG.

Therefore, the CFG remains the same:
```
S â†’ AxA | By | zC
A â†’ B | a
B â†’ C | b
C â†’ SS | c | Î» | ZBC
Z â†’ aBZ | BC | Z
```
